  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_7f935c5f-399c-4fa7-b253-5730063d6364'
      });
    }

      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"

          if (e.getStyle('display') !== 'none') {

            swapContent(lessLink);
          }
        });

<span id="freeTextContainer18043227018003482008"><b>چراغ‌ها را من خاموش می‌کنم = I Will Turn Off the Lights, Zoya Pirzad</b><br/><br/>Zoya Pirzad (Armenian: Զոյա Փիրզադ; born 1952 in Abadan) is an Iranian-Armenian writer and novelist. Her mother is Iranian Armenians and her father is of Russian background. <br/><br/>Deep in an Iranian suburb, made rich by the booming oil industry, Clarice Ayvazian lives a comfortable life surrounded by the gentle bickering of her children and her gossiping friends and relatives. <br/><br/>Happy being at the heart of her family, she devotes hersel</span>
  <span id="freeText18043227018003482008" style="display:none"><b>چراغ‌ها را من خاموش می‌کنم = I Will Turn Off the Lights, Zoya Pirzad</b><br /><br />Zoya Pirzad (Armenian: Զոյա Փիրզադ; born 1952 in Abadan) is an Iranian-Armenian writer and novelist. Her mother is Iranian Armenians and her father is of Russian background. <br /><br />Deep in an Iranian suburb, made rich by the booming oil industry, Clarice Ayvazian lives a comfortable life surrounded by the gentle bickering of her children and her gossiping friends and relatives. <br /><br />Happy being at the heart of her family, she devotes herself to their every need. But when an enigmatic Armenian family move in across the street, something begins to gnaw at Clarice's contentment: a feeling that there may be more to life – and to her – than this. <br /><br />Dizzy with the sweltering heat and simmering emotions, Clarice begins to feel herself come alive to possibilities previously unimaginable. Set in Iran prior to the Islamic revolution, Zoya Pirzad's award-winning novel crafts an intimate portrait of family life – its joys and its compromises – and how we find a happiness that endures.<br /><br />تاریخ نخستین خوانش: سال 2007میلادی<br /><br />عنوان: چراغ‌ها را من خاموش می‌کنم؛ نویسنده: زویا پیرزاد؛ مشخصات نشر تهران، نشر مرکز، 1380، در 294ص، شابک 9789643056568؛ موضوع: داستانهای نویسندگان ایرانی سده 14هجری خورشیدی، سده 21م<br /><br />جایزه های ادبی: بهترین رمان سال پکا - مهرگان ادب (1380هجری خورشیدی)، بهترین رمان سال بنیاد گلشیری (1380هجری خورشیدی)، بهترین رمان سال در بیستمین دوره ی کتاب سال جمهوری اسلامی ایران (1380هجری خورشیدی)، لوح تقدیر از نخستین دوره جایزه ی ادبی یلدا<br /><br />داستان کتاب به داستانهای راستین میماند، ولی واقعی نیست، و خیال نگارنده است؛ «چراغ‌ها را من خاموش می‌کنم» عنوان رمانی ایرانی، به قلم بانو «زویا پیرزاد» است، که توسط نشر «مرکز»، نخستین بار در سال 1380هجری خورشیدی منتشر شد، و تا کنون بیش از هفتاد بار تجدید چاپ شده است؛ علت نامگذاری کتاب را، میتوان نقش زن، به عنوان محور اصلی خانواده، و به عنوان خاموش کننده ی چراغها دانست؛ داستان در دهه ی 1340هجری خورشیدی، در محله ی «بوارده»ی «آبادان» رخ می‌دهد؛ راوی داستان، زنی «ارمنی» است، به نام «کلاریس آیوازیان»، که در این داستان، از روابط خانوادگی خویش، فرزندان دو قلو، و دنیای عاطفی آنها، و از همسایه‌ هایی سخن می‌گوید، که در «آبادان ـ در خانه‌ های سازمانی» زندگی می‌کنند؛ تلاش برای انس گرفتن با محیط، بن مایه ی دیگر داستان است؛ موضوع اصلی «یکنواختی زندگی یک زن خانه دار»، و «خستگی» او از آن روند، و «دل بستن به مرد همسایه»ای، که فکر میکند دنیای بهتری برایش به ارمغان خواهد آورد، و در موازات آن، گریزی زده میشود، به اوضاع سیاسی آن روزها، و تفکرات اجتماعی مردمان در آن سالها؛ ...؛<br /><br />تاریخ بهنگام رسانی 24/07/1399هجری خورشیدی؛ ا. شربیانی</span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Behnaz" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Behnaz" href="/user/show/27287713-behnaz">Behnaz</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781851689255" href="/book/show/13373135-things-we-left-unsaid">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27287713-behnaz?shelf=the-ones-i-loved">the-ones-i-loved</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27287713-behnaz?shelf=gioco-di-squadra">gioco-di-squadra</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27287713-behnaz?shelf=persian">persian</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1392353214" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9215652625041370051">After the armenian genocide lots of armenian people decided to emigrate. Their main destination was Iran. Even nowdays a considerable number of people in Iran are of Armenian descent. <br/>The story takes place in Abadan ( before the islamic revolution and therefore before the Iran-Iraq war ). Abadan nowdays is not as modernized as described in the book. Even though the war took place many years ago, Abadan could never reach the high levels it once had.<br/>Although I'm iranian, I've never been to the sou</span>
  <span id="freeText9215652625041370051" style="display:none">After the armenian genocide lots of armenian people decided to emigrate. Their main destination was Iran. Even nowdays a considerable number of people in Iran are of Armenian descent. <br />The story takes place in Abadan ( before the islamic revolution and therefore before the Iran-Iraq war ). Abadan nowdays is not as modernized as described in the book. Even though the war took place many years ago, Abadan could never reach the high levels it once had.<br />Although I'm iranian, I've never been to the southern part and I had no clue that living in Abadan could be like living in Europe. I've Always seen Abadan in movies about war, so this was the first thing that took me by surprise.<br />The book was as flowing as a river and I could imagine Clarisse actually talking to me with a cup of tea in her hands. Lots of people don't like books in which " it doesn't really happen anything". Well, I'm not one of those! The story and the style of Pirzad made me enter in the routine life of an armenian family, I felt fed up as Clarisse did when literally nothing "exciting" happend in her life and God how I was mad at her sister trying to find a husband and of course I was jealous of Violet, the perfectly beautiful but innocent woman who won the heart of Emil, the neighbour of Clarisse.<br />Another fantastic thing about the books in which "nothing happens" is that even secondary characters can share their stories ( for example the love story of Elmira Simonian or the lady talking about the armenian genocide, or Tigran ). Through the eyes of Clarisse we learn who they are but through their own words we have the opportunity to know who/what made them be like this.<br />The relationship between Emil and Clarisse is the most exciting thing in the book. and surprise!!!! There is no relationship. There is no affair going on between them. It's just the imagination of a lonely woman trying so hard to feel alive. <br />There are books that when you are done reading them, you are 100% sure you're going to read them again. This is one of those. </span>
  <a data-text-id="9215652625041370051" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sara" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sara" href="/user/show/408650-sara">Sara</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781851689255" href="/book/show/13373135-things-we-left-unsaid">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer420798557" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6777574081576640647">I was very curious to read a novel from an Armenian Iranian perspective, but unfortunately this book did not give much of a sense of their dynamics as a minority group in Iran. The writing style was simplistic, but this may be a result of poor translation. The main character Clarice lacks emotional depth and as a result almost reads as whiny or irritating by the end. I'm not one that requires resolution in the end of stories, but this book felt like it was leading toward one and then stops--I do</span>
  <span id="freeText6777574081576640647" style="display:none">I was very curious to read a novel from an Armenian Iranian perspective, but unfortunately this book did not give much of a sense of their dynamics as a minority group in Iran. The writing style was simplistic, but this may be a result of poor translation. The main character Clarice lacks emotional depth and as a result almost reads as whiny or irritating by the end. I'm not one that requires resolution in the end of stories, but this book felt like it was leading toward one and then stops--I don't want to give a spoiler so I'll just say that dramas or tensions just disappear. As a reader I'm left questioning what the author wanted me to experience or glean from the story with out a strong sense of place, depth of characters or a resolution or "lesson" to the story.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6777574081576640647" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2357222129" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2357222129" />
    <a title="Behrooz Parhami" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/61557581-behrooz-parhami"><img alt="Behrooz Parhami" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1482305944p2/61557581.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2357222129?book_show_action=true">Jun 08, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Behrooz Parhami" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Behrooz Parhami" href="/user/show/61557581-behrooz-parhami">Behrooz Parhami</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2357222129" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7679350448594725199">This acclaimed book was recommended and loaned to me by my sister Farnaz. As in Pirzad’s later novel, <i>We’ll Get Used to It</i> (my review: <a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2357221581'>https://www.goodreads.com/review/show...</a>), the writing consists of short, colloquial sentences. <i>The Lights</i> is better-written than <i>Get Used</i>, yet following the narrative, which comes in fits and starts, wasn’t easy for me. I guess this is the way people talk, yet one expects a novelist to do more than just string together short, superficial sentences.<br/><br/>This is a nove</span>
  <span id="freeText7679350448594725199" style="display:none">This acclaimed book was recommended and loaned to me by my sister Farnaz. As in Pirzad’s later novel, <i>We’ll Get Used to It</i> (my review: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2357221581">https://www.goodreads.com/review/show...</a>), the writing consists of short, colloquial sentences. <i>The Lights</i> is better-written than <i>Get Used</i>, yet following the narrative, which comes in fits and starts, wasn’t easy for me. I guess this is the way people talk, yet one expects a novelist to do more than just string together short, superficial sentences.<br /><br />This is a novel with realistic characters for its setting, although the author warns us at the outset that she has taken some liberties in shaping the locales where events happen. There is much to learn from this book about the lifestyles of the Armenian minority in Iran and the more or less Westernized oil city of Abadan in the southwestern province of Khuzestan, as it was before the Islamic Revolution and during the subsequent war with Iraq.<br /><br />The story is told from the vantage point of Claris, a young Armenian woman living with her husband Artush and three kids in Abadan of the 1960s. Their residence is part of the housing development where National Iranian Oil Company’s employees live. The family’s drab and relatively peaceful existence is disturbed when new neighbors move in. <br /><br />Claris, whose Latin-based name means “fame, most famous, most bright,” plays the role of a standard-issue, selfless, devoted housewife, but, deep down, she craves much more. She reads books and has a secret crush on a family friend, Emil, who visits from time to time. As a married woman in a traditional society, Claris keeps this taboo attraction under wraps, not mustering the courage to admit it even to herself. She thinks about this forbidden love nightly, for a few moments, as she gets ready to go to bed and announces that she will turn off the lights.<br /><br />Claris craves attention, an amenity that was in short supply for Iranian women living many decades ago. Artush and others bring home guests unannounced and Claris is expected to feed and pamper them. Once, she rebels and in answer to her husband’s “What’s for dinner?” question, replies “Nothing!” and suggests that they order food from the Annex (pp. 163-164). However, by and large, she plays the role of an obedient wife, curbing her rebellion and keeping illicit thoughts to herself.<br /><br />As I wrote in my review of <i>Get Used</i>, I am attracted to the works of Iranian women in view of their out-size influence on the political and rights movements in Iran and the attendant perspectives they bring to the ills of a backward, patriarchal society. Iranian women authors deserve to be read and heard, as they offer their diverse and compelling narratives about women’s predicaments and their struggle for independence and justice.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7679350448594725199" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1771212837" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1771212837" />
    <a title="Kirsty" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/56491386-kirsty"><img alt="Kirsty" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1485941532p2/56491386.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1771212837?book_show_action=true">Sep 29, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kirsty" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kirsty" href="/user/show/56491386-kirsty">Kirsty</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781851689255" href="/book/show/13373135-things-we-left-unsaid">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1771212837" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10962864969274453408">The novel uses the first person perspective of middle-aged housewife Clarice Ayvazian, who lives in the city of Abadan in Iran. We are launched into her suburban life from the book’s first sentence: ‘The sound of the school bus braking… the squeaking of the metal gate swinging open… the footsteps running up the narrow path across the front yard’.<br/><br/>Clarice is a stay-at-home mother to teenage son Armen, who is forever getting into ‘scuffles’ with his peers and who worries his parents when he begins </span>
  <span id="freeText10962864969274453408" style="display:none">The novel uses the first person perspective of middle-aged housewife Clarice Ayvazian, who lives in the city of Abadan in Iran. We are launched into her suburban life from the book’s first sentence: ‘The sound of the school bus braking… the squeaking of the metal gate swinging open… the footsteps running up the narrow path across the front yard’.<br /><br />Clarice is a stay-at-home mother to teenage son Armen, who is forever getting into ‘scuffles’ with his peers and who worries his parents when he begins to bathe on a regular basis, and younger twin girls named Armineh and Arsineh, both of whom have ‘chubby pink and white faces’. The rather happy monotony of Clarice’s life is broken with the sudden arrival of an Armenian family who move into the house across the street. This house is notorious for having a succession of different residents, all of whom have children that make friends with the twins and then leave without much warning. The new family consists of a father named Emile who has a job at the same engineering works as Clarice’s husband Artoush, his daughter Emily and her grandmother, Elmira Simonian.<br /><br />The narrative style of <i>Things We Left Unsaid</i> has been written in rather a chatty manner which launches straight into the finer points of Clarice’s life, mundane details which she alone seems to have any thought for. Parts of her narrative voice present mantra-like sentences in which she schools herself to be as accepted as possible by her family and neighbours: ‘So even if your mother and sister laugh, or your friends and acquaintances make remarks about how Clarice’s kitchen reminds them of the witch’s hut in Hansel and Gretel, you shouldn’t change your taste to accommodate theirs’.<br /><br />Although she is the novel’s protagonist and we learn more about her than anyone else, Clarice is not a likeable character. She comes across as rather selfish and perceives that her children take up the time which she would like to keep to herself. She does not seem to take into account that she is alone all day whilst her family is elsewhere, and some sympathy is created for the children as a result.<br /><br />The names of the characters are too similar at times – Emile, Emily and Elmira, and Armen, Artoush, Armineh and Arsineh, for example – and it would have been far better if these had been varied somewhat. At first, it is a little confusing as to which character is which and, although the twins are the most interesting individuals in the book – indeed, they are the only ones who seem to come to life – it is often difficult to tell them apart.<br /><br />Throughout, there is a definite sense that the book has not been translated perhaps as well as it could have been, and many Americanisms are reverted to which seem to alienate the reader from the cultural aspect of the novel. Examples of this are that the twins wear ‘white collar strings’, a phrase which is never elaborated upon, and various references are made to the girls being ‘Xeroxed copies of one another’, school ‘recess’ breaks, ‘blocks’ of residence and ‘a zillion insects’. Such translations do not really work, especially with regard to the book’s setting.<br /><br /><i>Things We Left Unsaid</i> includes a glossary which is made up of places and the proper names of characters and real people referred to throughout, as well as foodstuffs which ‘Anglophone readers would not generally find familiar’. Rather than just listing translations for each of the more unfamiliar words, a small paragraph has been included alongside each entry, which explains the history of a particular place, person or point in history.<br /><br />Whilst the setting of the book and the period of history which is its focus – just before the Islamic Revolution – are incredibly interesting, there is nothing which really serves to get the reader interested in the story. <i>Things We Left Unsaid</i> is essentially a slim portrait of family life which never really goes any deeper than its surface. It feels dull and repetitive at times, and the entire novel feels as though it has been comprised of the more lulling episodes of a soap opera, where the characters merely meander along and where nothing of any substance occurs.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10962864969274453408" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2759487840" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2586017210169158607">I read this book in translation. As a native of the country the author is from, I found the translation fell short at times. So that's the only reason I am giving it two stars.<br/>I liked the story. Clarice, the protagonist, is a full time mom and housewife. She has three children, the twins Arsineh, Armineh, and her son Armen. Her husband, Artoush, is a man with strong political convictions, something Clarice openly objects. Her mother is a perfectionist, and she is not shy in pointing out to Clari</span>
  <span id="freeText2586017210169158607" style="display:none">I read this book in translation. As a native of the country the author is from, I found the translation fell short at times. So that's the only reason I am giving it two stars.<br />I liked the story. Clarice, the protagonist, is a full time mom and housewife. She has three children, the twins Arsineh, Armineh, and her son Armen. Her husband, Artoush, is a man with strong political convictions, something Clarice openly objects. Her mother is a perfectionist, and she is not shy in pointing out to Clarice her shortcomings in being a housewife. Her sister, Alice, is her complete opposite. She thinks highly of herself, has too much self confidence, and has an opinion about anyone and anything (more often unfavorable).<br />The story is almost insignificant; recounts the daily lives of Clarice's family, and her social interactions with friends and neighbor. Through out these insignificant events we meet the new neighbors, Emile, a widower, his daughter, Emily, and his mother. They add an element of enigma to the story of Clarice's life. Clarice interacts with them, almost reluctantly, and only because others wants her to do so. Ironically, this forced relationship opens Clarice up to a whole new level of self awareness. She questions her current status and wonders about the choices she has made in life.<br />I could empathize with Clarice. I too often find myself putting everyone else's needs ahead of mine. But, I hoped the story offered a shift, but instead, I think, it offered a middle ground, where Clarice was still the same person, doing the same things, but now, recognized [instead of taken for granted].</span>
  <a data-text-id="2586017210169158607" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText9324493063486468632" style="display:none">This is my first book written by the author, Zoya Pirzad. I rated is 4/5. I will definitely read another book by the author. It’s a light read. I finished it in two days.</span>

<div id="review_3275773113" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3275773113" />
    <a title="Angela" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22046168-angela"><img alt="Angela" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1504687930p2/22046168.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3275773113?book_show_action=true">Apr 11, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Angela" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Angela" href="/user/show/22046168-angela">Angela</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781851689255" href="/book/show/13373135-things-we-left-unsaid">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3275773113" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11095861656283686544">I really enjoyed this book, especially the author's ability to make you feel the emotions bottled up inside the main character. So many things she thought but didn't say out loud frustrated me, mainly because it goes against my own outspoken nature. Yet, there were times that I guess her inability to vocalize was actually her saving grace. It was satisfying to see the subtle transformations she, and those around her went through.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2966719126" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2966719126" />
    <a title="Samira" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/42288633-samira"><img alt="Samira" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1568150264p2/42288633.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2966719126?book_show_action=true">Sep 06, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Samira" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Samira" href="/user/show/42288633-samira">Samira</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/42288633-samira?shelf=read-in-2019">read-in-2019</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/42288633-samira?shelf=persian-novels">persian-novels</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2966719126" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12811026802192431517">I wanted to give this book a 3-star rating. I did at first, but my conscience cannot allow it anymore. So the actual rating falls on 2.5 stars.<br/><br/>I really wanted to love this book. I started it with high expectations and it was good. I did like the writhing style, the story, and the perspective of an Armenian woman in Iran. The story was fast and very easy to read. But there was a big problem. For me at least.<br/><br/>I thought this book was about a woman who realizes she has never really lived for herself.</span>
  <span id="freeText12811026802192431517" style="display:none">I wanted to give this book a 3-star rating. I did at first, but my conscience cannot allow it anymore. So the actual rating falls on 2.5 stars.<br /><br />I really wanted to love this book. I started it with high expectations and it was good. I did like the writhing style, the story, and the perspective of an Armenian woman in Iran. The story was fast and very easy to read. But there was a big problem. For me at least.<br /><br />I thought this book was about a woman who realizes she has never really lived for herself. She has always done what is expected of her and kept her head out of trouble. She does her duties as a wife and a mother. And it IS about that. Clarice realizes that she is not really satisfied with her life. Here's where the problem starts. She never says anything. She avoids conversations altogether, avoids conflicts, and avoids voicing her thoughts (because her father advised her to do that,) and then she blames others for never knowing that she also needs something (she also does that in her head, not aloud). Now, that's also fine in the starting point. But Clarice never does anything to change that throughout the story. She doesn't really take any considerable action. She never talks to her husband or her family and remains a passive character until everyone changes suddenly and then everything is good. I expected more. I expected her to do something before the end. She does start doing something new in the end but that is after everyone has already changed their behavior and she hasn't said a word about it. <br /><br />As a woman living in Iran, I am faced with this problem everyday. I see it in older generation women in my life and I don't think women in Iran need anymore encouragement to be more passive. People can't read your mind if you never say anything. I don't think women should be silent in voicing their feelings to this extent. You should say so if something is wrong not be proper and good and never say a word till a miracle happens. <br /><br />Again I was really excited about this book because I thought Clarice is gonna take a stand against the old-fashioned culture around her, but I was disappointed. Still the book had potential and it was enjoyable to some extent but I wished for more. It's a different read among Persian books and I think people can still enjoy it despite all that.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12811026802192431517" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_325690346" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/325690346" />
    <a title="cheryl" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1886600-cheryl"><img alt="cheryl" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1547331396p2/1886600.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/325690346?book_show_action=true">May 07, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="cheryl" class="user" itemprop="url" name="cheryl" href="/user/show/1886600-cheryl">cheryl</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781851689255" href="/book/show/13373135-things-we-left-unsaid">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer325690346" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer261628471050749800">I was a Goodreads giveaway winner for this recently released translation of a novel by an Iranian-Armenian author. It is set in Iran in 1962 and focuses on a town built around an oil company and an Armenian community with it's own social groups, religious institutions (notably, everyone has Friday off because it it the day of worship in the country but it is not the day of worship in the characters' religion), and schools. The main character is Clarisse, a wife and mother to a teenage son and yo</span>
  <span id="freeText261628471050749800" style="display:none">I was a Goodreads giveaway winner for this recently released translation of a novel by an Iranian-Armenian author. It is set in Iran in 1962 and focuses on a town built around an oil company and an Armenian community with it's own social groups, religious institutions (notably, everyone has Friday off because it it the day of worship in the country but it is not the day of worship in the characters' religion), and schools. The main character is Clarisse, a wife and mother to a teenage son and younger twin girls. Her mother and sister (who is perpetually looking for a husband herself) are frequent, usually unannounced, visitors to her home. The book opens when the children bring home a new friend who has moved into a neighboring home with her father and grandmother. As Clarisse gets to know her neighbors and navigates her changing community (we see hints of a women's movement), she begins to question her life and her marriage, wondering for what seems to be the first time if she is happy and fulfilled. <br /><br /><br />I greatly enjoyed this book. In many ways, the basic story of a woman questioning her life, a life she's just lived for many years as a wife and mother without really examining her own satisfaction, could be set anywhere. However, the culture definitely runs throughout and I enjoyed the glimpses into a different society. The Armenians in Iran are very much a subculture and they generally only interact within their own community. The community in the book is also very much built around the oil company, it is a more institutionalized version of the company towns we see in the U.S. with housing and transport built for workers and with different neighborhoods for workers and management (again, something seen in many U.S. towns). It isn't a major focal point, but there are hints of politics such as when Clarisse wanders into a speech about women's rights (she's drawn to it but also very uncomfortable) and her concerns that her husband's political interests might be dangerous.<br /><br /><br />Through a good portion of the book, it was a hinting at being a 5 star read for me. As it progressed, I got a bit frustrated with Clarisse and I felt like I couldn't really relate to her sudden discontent. I felt like she was a bit dramatic at times, almost acting like things were tragic. I don't want to spell out more detail since it might be spoiler-ish and think the disconnect may be due to cultural/temporal differences. Despite that issue, I very much enjoyed the book and give it 4.5...rounding down just b/c I'm stingy with putting 5. I enjoyed the writing voice, though I never know how much of that to attribute to a writer and how much a translator influenced a book that originated in another language.<br /><br /><br />FYI -- There is a useful appendix in the version I have that explains some elements such as public figures, cultural observations, and even foods that an American reader might not recognize. I found it helpful to actually read through this section all at once (stumbled upon it when I looked to see how many pages were in the novel, an odd habit of mine) and then consulted it a couple of times as I read. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="261628471050749800" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Zenochka" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Zenochka" href="/user/show/26878846-zenochka">Zenochka</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781851689255" href="/book/show/13373135-things-we-left-unsaid">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26878846-zenochka?shelf=iran">iran</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26878846-zenochka?shelf=armenia">armenia</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1162058179" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17470733393090609756">Having read Pirzad's novel 'The space between us,' I had relatively high hopes for this one. The book is translated mainly from the Persian and, without sounding cliched, much is lost in translation. The book is mainly dialogue and in my opinion, Persian and Armenian colloquial and conversational phrases cannot accurately be translated into English. It would be interesting to read the book in its original languages. The novel delves into the life of your average 'bourgeois' housewife in pre-revo</span>
  <span id="freeText17470733393090609756" style="display:none">Having read Pirzad's novel 'The space between us,' I had relatively high hopes for this one. The book is translated mainly from the Persian and, without sounding cliched, much is lost in translation. The book is mainly dialogue and in my opinion, Persian and Armenian colloquial and conversational phrases cannot accurately be translated into English. It would be interesting to read the book in its original languages. The novel delves into the life of your average 'bourgeois' housewife in pre-revolution Iran, except that she's Armenian. The plot itself requires rumination to be understood and the imagery used by Pirzad allows for many interpretations, making it an ok read. All in all, I enjoyed it at times but grew frustrated with Clarice's complete lack of ambition! Surely after 15-odd years of marriage, you'd think you'd do more than wash the drapes and cook fesenjoun all day.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17470733393090609756" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('uyUwr1o1ATCKXEhdKG5dym3QspNOtIQ/ruC2qyLK6z5HI7QB1GfybsOndAF3dPOa/oq3D2OxZ+0NiWC5177bjA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('4wdbmOq2Bx7mkwz+9Gj9uNXTN0XXNiMQ/Z7IycDoz3cfAd82ZOT0QK9oMKKrclPoRoky2fozwMJe9x7bNZz/xQ==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

<span id="freeTextContainer828406595245576242">Zoya Pirzad is a renowned Iranian-Armenian writer and novelist. She is the author of the international bestseller Things We Left Unsaid, and her most recent collection of stories, The Bitter Taste of Persimmon, won the prize for Best Foreign Book of 2009 in France.<br/><br/>زویا پیرزاد نویسنده و داستان نویس معاصر در سال ۱۳۳۱ در آبادان از مادری ارمنی تبار و پدری روس تبار به دنیا آمد. در همان جا به مدرسه رفت </span>
  <span id="freeText828406595245576242" style="display:none">Zoya Pirzad is a renowned Iranian-Armenian writer and novelist. She is the author of the international bestseller Things We Left Unsaid, and her most recent collection of stories, The Bitter Taste of Persimmon, won the prize for Best Foreign Book of 2009 in France.<br /><br />زویا پیرزاد نویسنده و داستان نویس معاصر در سال ۱۳۳۱ در آبادان از مادری ارمنی تبار و پدری روس تبار به دنیا آمد. در همان جا به مدرسه رفت و در تهران ازدواج کرد و دو پسرش ساشا و شروین را به دنیا آورد<br /></span>
